Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In supramolecular host frameworks, multiple pyrrole units can be strategically arranged to create a binding pocket or cavity that is complementary in size, shape, and charge to target anions. The NH groups of pyrrole can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anions such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate, stabilizing the host-guest complex. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can further stabilize the interaction with anions. The modularity of supramolecular design enables the tuning of host geometry and binding site preorganization, enhancing selectivity and affinity for specific anions. This combination of hydrogen bonding capability, structural preorganization, and electronic effects makes pyrrole-containing hosts highly effective in anion recognition and binding, a principle widely In supramolecular host frameworks, multiple pyrrole units can be strategically arranged to create a binding pocket or cavity that is complementary in size, shape, and charge to target anions. The NH groups of pyrrole can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anions such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate, stabilizing the host-guest complex. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can further stabilize the interaction with anions. In supramolecular host frameworks, multiple pyrrole units can be strategically arranged to create a binding pocket or cavity that is complementary in size, shape, and charge to target anions. In supramolecular host frameworks, multiple pyrrole units can be strategically arranged to create a binding pocket or cavity that is complementary in size, shape, and charge to target anions. The NH groups of pyrrole can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anions such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate, stabilizing the host-guest complex. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can further stabilize the interaction with anions.
